    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="310e2105a42e4999ae639cc076519e19">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '7dada2c5-15d9-4578-890e-128466acb517'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        <div class="relative"><a href="#" data-db-target-for="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865" data-db-switch="icon-close_thin" aria-haspopup="true" aria-controls="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Pop" role="button" id="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Ctrl" aria-label="Tools" class="article-tools__ctrl"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Tools"></i><span>Tools</span></a><div data-db-target-of="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865" aria-labelledby="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Ctrl" id="0e406ce9-5c43-4e2d-b741-e0b43d1f1865_Pop" class="article-tools__block fixed dropBlock__holder"><ul role="menu" class="rlist w-slide--list"><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="https://advanced.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/action/rightsLink?doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202408642&amp;mode=" role="menuitem" target="_blank"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-permissions-key"></i><span>Request permission</span></a></li><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202408642" role="menuitem" target="_self"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-export-icon"></i><span>Export citation</span></a></li><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="/personalize/addFavoritePublication?doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202408642" role="menuitem" target="_self"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-favorites"></i><span>Add to favorites</span></a></li><li role="presentation" class="article-tool"><a href="/action/addCitationAlert?doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202408642" role="menuitem" target="_self"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Track-citations"></i><span>Track citation</span></a></li></ul></div></div>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d20949620" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium layered oxide cathodes can uniquely benefit from the existing lithium-ion battery industry as sodium-ion batteries gain traction as a potential low-cost, drop-in replacement. However, achieving relevant energy density with a suitable cycle life remains a challenge for sodium layered oxides. At high operating potentials, several competing degradation mechanisms prevent P2-type Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (550&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>1</sup>) from achieving meaningful cycle lifewith bulk structural instability and surface reactivity being the primary retractors. Herein, the issue of particle cracking is addressed through detailed synthesis methods of single-crystal materials. By comparison to a polycrystalline baseline, the single-crystal materials quantify the capacity loss due to isolation of active material caused by intergranular particle cracking. The single crystal materials are then employed in cells with an advanced, localized saturated electrolyte (LSE) to demonstrate the magnitude of capacity loss due to electrolyte decomposition at the cathode surface. Mitigation of the surface reactivity through the LSE electrolyte effectively demonstrates the elevated importance of surface reactivity at high voltages despite the onset of egregious particle cracking. This work aims to guide future research into understanding molten-salt assisted syntheses and advance the debate on surface versus bulk degradation.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202408642-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202408642-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The world is charging forward with more sustainable forms of energy, and electrochemical energy storage will be a necessary tool to enable a future powered by intermittent energy sources. Rechargeable batteries have emerged as an integral solution in the past 50 years with the commercialization of the lithium-ion battery (LIB) and the subsequent electrification of motorized vehicles (EVs).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0001_R_d20949610e328" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Commun.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Countries are already beginning to require total electrification of new vehicles by 2035.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0002_R_d20949610e333" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While lithium-ion technologies have been pivotal in enabling vehicle electrification and reducing reliance on fossil fuels, the growing cost and scarcity/extraction rate of lithium will limit the reach of its applications due to the vast storage capacity needed to enable renewable energy on a grid-scale basis.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0003_R_d20949610e338" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Science">3</a>-<a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0005_R_d20949610e338" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sodium-ion batteries are a viable alternative, as sodium itself is 1000 times more abundant in the Earth's crust and already extracted at 100 times the rate of Li<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, allowing for rapid industrialization of the cathode technology on a large scale.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0006_R_d20949610e348" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Sodium layered oxides, the analogous material to the industrialized lithium layered oxides, also benefit from the ability to use a much broader array of transition-metal compositions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0004_R_d20949610e355" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy Technol.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> One of the more promising compositions for immediate adoption is P2-type Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NM12), which uses a relatively low nickel (a costly transition metal) content compared to industrialized LiNi<sub>x</sub>Mn<sub>y</sub>Co<sub>1-x-y</sub>O<sub>2</sub>/LiNi<sub>x</sub>Mn<sub>y</sub>Al<sub>1-x-y</sub>O<sub>2</sub> compositions for LIBs. Here, P2 refers to Delmas notation, where Na<sup>+</sup> occupies prismatic sites (P) with two (2) transition metal layers per hexagonal unit cell.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0007_R_d20949610e388" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Phys. B+C">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The inclusion of Ni in this composition offers a unique advantage compared to other high manganese compositions due to the lying of the Ni<sup>2+/3+</sup>: e<sub>g</sub> band at a lower energy compared to the Mn<sup>3+/4+</sup>: e<sub>g</sub> band and consequent higher operating voltage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0001_R_d20949610e402" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Commun.">1</a>, <a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0004_R_d20949610e402" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy Technol.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Accounting for the Na vacancies in the structure, the average oxidation states will be Ni<sup>2+</sup> and Mn<sup>4+</sup>, which avoids the Jahn-Teller active trivalent ions. Ni<sup>3+</sup> is highly reactive with ambient moisture, thus synthesizing the material with nickel in its divalent state helps make NM12 incredibly air stable for a sodium layered oxide and allows for synthesis in air.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0008_R_d20949610e414" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Chem. Mater.">8</a>-<a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0010_R_d20949610e414" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Storage Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In terms of electrochemical performance, this material is extremely stable in the potential range of 2.5  3.8&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> where only the Ni<sup>2+/3+</sup> redox is accessed and no phase transition is observed. While over 1000 cycles can be achieved at a 1C charge/discharge rate, the capacity is relatively low at only 80&nbsp;mA h g<sup>1</sup> initially.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0011_R_d20949610e427" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Storage Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>When more sodium is extracted, the Ni<sup>3+/4+</sup> redox (as well as some oxygen redox) is accessed around 4.15&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>, achieving an initial capacity at 2.5  4.3&nbsp;V of 145&nbsp;mA h g<sup>1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0011_R_d20949610e440" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Storage Mater.">11</a>-<a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0013_R_d20949610e440" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Chem. Commun.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This increases the average operating potential to a relatively high 3.65&nbsp;V in half cells with sodium-metal anode. Above 4.15&nbsp;V against Na/Na<sup>+</sup>, the additional capacity NM12 gains are highly irreversible as half cells with this cathode material fall below 80% retention in roughly 10 cycles at a rate of C/5. NM12 undergoes a P2  O2 phase transition that is achieved by gliding of the oxygen slabs. This transition readily creates stacking faults and has been attributed to significant generation and growth of intragranular microcracks along the (002) plane and problems associated with oxygen redox.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0014_R_d20949610e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - ACS Cent. Sci.">14</a>, <a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0015_R_d20949610e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nat. Commun.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, unlike in lithium layered oxides that experience intragranular cracking, i.e., LiNi<sub>0.8</sub>Mn<sub>0.1</sub>Co<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, microcracks nucleate at the surface in Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> particles instead of within the bulk, leaving isolated lamellas behind.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0012_R_d20949610e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nano Energy">12</a>, <a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0016_R_d20949610e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Sustain. Mater. Technol.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Gliding has similarly been shown to be reversible in some lithium compositions, and further exploration on degradation mechanisms for the P2  O2 phase transition is needed.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0017_R_d20949610e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Science">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To date, syntheses of NM12 have employed materials with many polycrystalline agglomerates formed during traditional solid-state syntheses of the material.</p>
         
         <p>Drawing from recent research in lithium layered oxides, there has been significant interest in forming single crystals of cathode material as a way of mitigating capacity loss due to particle cracking, specifically intergranular cracking.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0018_R_d20949610e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Storage Mater.">18</a>-<a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0021_R_d20949610e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Ionics (Kiel)">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Here, the term single crystal is used more colloquially to refer to large isolated primary particles. There are a variety of methods that can be used to produce single crystals, but of particular interest is molten-salt methods that employ flux salts, such as NaCl, in the synthesis to promote dissolution and recrystallization of the active material during synthesis. For lithium layered oxides, molten-salt compositions have often required complex mixtures containing expensive and undesirable salts, such as CsCl, to form the desired phase and particle morphology.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0020_R_d20949610e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">20</a>, <a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0022_R_d20949610e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">22</a>, <a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0023_R_d20949610e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Mater. Chem. A">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At the higher temperatures often required for sodium layered oxide calcinations, salts like CsCl and LiCl with lower boiling points will be more volatile leading to evaporation of corrosive material. Tunability of particle morphology is desirable to expose less reactive surface facets, and meticulous exploration of the molten-salt fluxes is required to alter the morphology if it is possible to change.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0023_R_d20949610e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Mater. Chem. A">23</a>, <a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0024_R_d20949610e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While the synthesis of single-crystal sodium layered-oxides may itself be difficult, the composition of molten-salts should also be limited to fit the appeal of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) as a low-cost, abundant alternative to LIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Alterative single-crystal synthesis methods have made use of spinel-phase intermediates that contain lithium within the structure, as spinel materials have been shown to readily form single crystals in the form of truncated octahedra.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0025_R_d20949610e504" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Mater. Chem. A">25</a>-<a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0027_R_d20949610e504" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Mater. Chem. A">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Our previous work demonstrated that this can be an effective approach for sodium layered oxides by using the mixed transition-metal spinel Ni<sub>1.5</sub>Mn<sub>1.5</sub>O<sub>4</sub> as a precursor to form single crystal O3-type NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0028_R_d20949610e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Single crystal research for sodium layered oxides is still in its infancy compared to lithium analogs, and much work is needed to understand the different synthesis routes that can be used to produce single crystals. However, the motivation for this work is clear as within the past few years there have been several reports of single-crystal sodium layered oxide materials with improved electrochemical performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0029_R_d20949610e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Small">29</a>, <a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0030_R_d20949610e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - New J. Chem.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> There is similar interest in applications of single-crystal layered oxides to all solid-state batteries, though much work is still needed on sodium solid-state electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0031_R_d20949610e533" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Small">31</a>-<a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0033_R_d20949610e533" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Curr. Opin. Electrochem.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Recently, a molten-salt assisted synthesis of P2-type NZM (Na<sub>0.66</sub>Ni<sub>0.26</sub>Zn<sub>0.07</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>) has also been reported.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0034_R_d20949610e549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The authors include zinc in their composition to avoid the P2  O2 phase transition experienced in NM12 and instead undergo a P2  OP4 transition, greatly improving capacity retention though at a significant cost of 30&nbsp;mA h g<sup>1</sup> in capacity.</p>
         
         <p>By forming single crystals of undoped Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, the effects of intergranular and intragranular particle cracking on capacity decay can be deconvoluted when compared to a control sample containing many polycrystalline agglomerates synthesized by a solid-state method. This will elucidate the true detriment of the P2  O2 phase transition and the associated planar delamination of active material particles on capacity retentiona necessary step to deconvolute the influence of surface reactivity and anisotropic volume changes on capacity fade in sodium layered oxides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0035_R_d20949610e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Joule">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Due to the poor mechanical stability of NM12, this may also prove to be an upper limit on the performance benefit of single-crystal Na<sub>x</sub>MO<sub>2</sub> materials that naturally crystallize with platelet morphology.</p>
         
         <p>Once intergranular particle cracking is controlled by use of single-crystal particles, we will be able to probe the question of whether intragranular cracking is the primary cause of capacity fade in NM12 at high voltages. While the demonstrated extent of particle cracking in literature is egregious, the interaction between propylene carbonate and NM12 offers a competing instability at the highly oxidative potentials corresponding to the P2  O2 phase transition. Cracking can serve to exacerbate chemical instabilities by routinely exposing new surfaces where oxygen layer gliding introduces stacking faults. Transition-metal dissolution also occurs as a mechanism of crack propagation, exposing oxygen to the crack interface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0012_R_d20949610e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nano Energy">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Previous research from our group into an advanced class of electrolytes known as localized saturated electrolyte (LSE) with lithium layered oxide systems presents a compelling opportunity for study within a sodium-ion system.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0036_R_d20949610e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Mater. Chem. A">36</a>-<a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0039_R_d20949610e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Lithium based LSEs have typically constituted LiPF<sub>6</sub> as an inexpensive alternative to lithium (bis)fluorosulfonylimide (LiFSI), which is primarily used as the salt in localized high concentration electrolytes (LHCEs) that rely on dimethoxyethane (DME) as a solvent.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0040_R_d20949610e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The solvation structure of LSEs achieve improved cycling stability with LiMO<sub>2</sub> (M = transition metal) by increasing the coordination of the carbonate solvent to LiPF<sub>6</sub>, thereby increasing the LUMO level of the salt-solvent pair, increasing the oxidative stability of the electrolyte, and preventing parasitic surface reactions with LiMO<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0037_R_d20949610e606" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Energy Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Synchrotron total X-ray scattering and small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS) showed that solutions of LiPF<sub>6</sub> near their saturation limit form contact-ion pairs (CIPs) and aggregates (AGGs) that result in increased coordination due to the limited free solvent concentration. By a similar argument, the HOMO level of the salt-solvent pair also decreases, improving the reductive stability on the lithium metal side.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0037_R_d20949610e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Energy Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Changes in the bond lengths of solvent/salt molecules and subsequently the LUMO/HOMO levels of the electrolyte were also verified by DFT calculations. These changes to the solvation structure of the electrolyte result in a wider electrochemical stability window and reduced solvent decomposition at high voltages.</p>
         
         <p>LSEs have offered improved stability up to 4.6&nbsp;V versus Li/Li<sup>+</sup>, which corresponds to roughly 4.3&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0039_R_d20949610e625" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While there are sparse reports in the literature on LHCEs with sodium layered oxides, the high potential of 4.3&nbsp;V has not been thoroughly studied with advanced electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0041_R_d20949610e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Nat. Energy">41</a>, <a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0042_R_d20949610e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> DME-based and even trimethyl/triethyl phosphate (TEP/TMP) based LHCEs become oxidatively unstable despite the design of the solvation structure at this high of a potential. An appropriately designed carbonate-based LSE with NaPF<sub>6</sub> salt for sodium-ion batteries could prove to assuage the compounding detriment of surface reactivity at high voltages.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we will discuss the use of both direct (flux-salt included during sodiation) and indirect (flux-salt use separated from sodiation step) molten-salt synthesis methods to form single-crystal particles of P2-type NM12.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0028_R_d20949610e642" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The electrochemical stability of single crystal NM12 materials is compared to a polycrystalline control to explore different effects of intragranular and intergranular particle cracking in NM12 at high voltages. Subsequently, single-crystal NM12 is paired with a novel NaPF<sub>6</sub>-based LSE against both Na metal and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> to gauge the chemical stability of NM12 at high potentials. From these results, we can deconstruct the significance of intergranular/intragranular particle cracking and surface reactivity/electrolyte stability as they relate to the rapid capacity fade observed at high voltages.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202408642-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202408642-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202408642-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202408642-sec-0030-title">2.1 Single-Crystal Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and Bulk Stability</h3>
            
            <p>Herein, we will discuss two different routes for the synthesis of single-crystal Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> particles. The first route requires two steps with the first being a molten-salt assisted synthesis of single-crystal NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> material, and the second being a calcination of said NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> material. The second route is a one-step calcination procedure aided by a molten-salt flux and proceeded by a brief washing step.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202408642-sec-0040">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="adfm202408642-sec-0040-title">2.1.1 Synthesis of NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> Spinel Precursors</h4>
               
               <p>As discussed in our previous work of an O3-type material, the existence of mixed transition-metal spinel phases allows for tunability of a precursor morphology for Na<sub>x</sub>MO<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0028_R_d20949610e713" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared to Ni<sub>1.5</sub>Mn<sub>1.5</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> is a more stable material in the form of a cubic inverse spinel, where all of the nickel will be in its divalent form and manganese will be present in some fraction as Mn<sup>2+</sup>, Mn<sup>3+</sup>, and Mn<sup>4+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0043_R_d20949610e736" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Spectrochim. Acta  Part A Mol. Biomol. Spectrosc.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Here, the morphology of NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> itself is studied by changing the flux salt composition as well as the size of the NiO and Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> particles used in the synthesis. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0001">1</a> show the effect of different synthesis conditions with unmilled NiO and Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, where the size of the NiO is 5&nbsp;m and the Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> is up to 20&nbsp;m (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Images of milled oxide particles are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information) for comparison. Without milling, solid-state diffusion of the transition metals is sluggish and conversion to the cubic NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> spinel phase is limited even after 10&nbsp;h at 825&nbsp;C. This can be seen by the varying particle sizes and morphologies in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0001">1a</a>, as well as the impurities presented in the X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="adfm202408642-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/caf7f64c-10cc-4bcb-97ac-779802c35a26/adfm202408642-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/caf7f64c-10cc-4bcb-97ac-779802c35a26/adfm202408642-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7a7f2028-5a51-419b-a066-c7e0e0f3b9c1/adfm202408642-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/caf7f64c-10cc-4bcb-97ac-779802c35a26/adfm202408642-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202408642-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202408642" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">SEM images of NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> synthesized under various conditions, starting with unmilled transition-metal oxide precursors: a) solid-state synthesis at 825&nbsp;C for 12&nbsp;h, b) molten-salt synthesis with a NaCl flux salt at 825&nbsp;C for 2&nbsp;h, c) molten-salt synthesis with a mixed A<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> flux (70: 30 by mol) at 825&nbsp;C for 2&nbsp;h, and d) molten-salt synthesis with a mixed A<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> flux (50: 50 by mol) at 825&nbsp;C for 2&nbsp;h. e) Particle size distributions for molten-salt synthesized samples.</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               <p>Conversely, full conversion to NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> is shown with only a 2&nbsp;h high temperature hold when using an NaCl flux (0.75 mols NaCl per mol NiO). These particles crystallize as truncated octahedra with an average size of 3.4&nbsp;m (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0001">1b</a>). NaCl is considered an ideal flux due to its high melting point (805&nbsp;C), relatively low chemical reactivity at high temperatures, and its low cost and ubiquity for practical applications. Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> is also an interesting flux candidate for similar reasons, but Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> decomposes close to its melting point, which is higher at 884&nbsp;C. Thus, Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>/ Li<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> binary salt mixtures (called A<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> from here on) are considered to decrease the melting temperature below 800&nbsp;C and prevent decomposition of the sulfate flux, where the Li<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> salt is recoverable after synthesis. Both a 70: 30&nbsp;mol ratio and a 50: 50&nbsp;mol ratio of Na: Li in A<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> are used as flux salts, again showing complete conversion to NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> with unmilled metal oxides in 2&nbsp;h at 825&nbsp;C. With a ratio of 0.50&nbsp;mol mixed sulfates per mol of NiO, these particles crystallize as polyhedra, both with an average size of 1.5&nbsp;m (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0001">1ce</a>). The 50: 50&nbsp;mol ratio sample shows reduced agglomeration relative to the 70: 30 sample, likely due to the lower melting point creating more time for dissolution into the flux.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0044_R_d20949610e862" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - J. Am. Ceram. Soc.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information) shows the particle morphology of NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> synthesized with a 25: 75 ratio of Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>: Li<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>, which has roughly the same melting temperature as the 70: 30 mixture. From this, it can be seen that the extent of agglomeration is worse than that in the 50: 50 mixture, yet better than that in the 70: 30 mixture, suggesting that agglomeration is both affected by the melting point and the alkali metal in A<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>. The reductive/oxidative nature of the two fluxes may also play a role in the cation distribution within the spinel as it is known to deviate from the simple Ni<sup>2+</sup> and Mn<sup>3+</sup>, although exploration of this would require advanced characterization, such as neutron diffraction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0045_R_d20949610e892" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - J. Alloys Compd.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
               
               <p>Ball milling can similarly be used to reduce the size of NiO and Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> prior to heat treatment and potentially tune the size of the NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> crystals. SEM micrographs resulting from different synthesis conditions are presented in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information) with corresponding XRD patterns contained within Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information), and Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information) is included to highlight the NiO impurities and lattice parameters calculated from Rietveld refinement of all the discussed NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> materials. Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information) compares the surface area of different NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> materials calculated from the multi-point Brunauer-Emmett-Teller (BET) theory of nitrogen physisorption. Syntheses utilizing milled oxides as the starting material for NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> are discussed more thoroughly in the Supporting Information. From here on, the 70: 30&nbsp;mol ratio of A<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> was used exclusively due to its lower lithium content.</p>
               </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202408642-sec-0050">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="adfm202408642-sec-0050-title">2.1.2 Synthesis of Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> from NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> Precursors (SC2)</h4>
               
               <p>The motivation for controlling the morphology of the NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> precursors is to subsequently control the morphology of Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> after a solid-state calcination step. This method is termed as the SC2 method owing to the two separated high temperature steps required to synthesize single-crystal NM12 with this route. The morphology of NM12 materials formed by calcining NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> precursors (at 900&nbsp;C for 10&nbsp;h with 5% excess NaOH) are shown in the SEM images in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information) with corresponding XRD patterns in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information) and calculated lattice parameters and NiO impurities summarized in Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). The morphological impact of these varied precursors is explored in detail in the Supporting Information. In summary, the tunability in the precursor morphology of NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> appears to only translate to the tunability of the hexagonal definition in the NM12 platelets, and possible explanations for this phenomenon will be discussed at the end of the next section. However, all SC2-NM12 materials were able to form relatively isolated primary particles, offering potential for single-crystal NM12 cathode composites with minimal secondary particle structure.</p>
               </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202408642-sec-0060">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="adfm202408642-sec-0060-title">2.1.3 Molten-Salt Assisted Synthesis of Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (SC1)</h4>
               
               <p>For Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, separation of the sodiation step from the use of a molten-salt is not necessary. The excellent moisture stability of NM12 means flux-salts remaining after calcination can be dissolved in water and filtered without damaging the layered structure. An annealing step after washing is also unnecessary; the material only needs to be sufficiently dried at 110&nbsp;C after performing vacuum filtration to remove the residual flux salts. Materials formed with this synthesis method are termed as SC1 as they require one high temperature step to form single crystals of NM12. Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a</a> (Supporting Information) is provided for verification of the moisture stability of NM12 during washing, where the powder was stored in deionized water at room temperature for a week (compared to only a 10&nbsp;min wash time), and then dried at 110&nbsp;C for 12&nbsp;h. XRD patterns collected after this treatment show minimal structural changes, and half cells similarly showed the expected first cycle capacity and cycling performance in the 2.4  4&nbsp;V window.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0046_R_d20949610e1019" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Nat. Commun.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
               
               <p>Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9b,c</a> (Supporting Information) compares NaCl volatilization for the SC1 and SC2 methods, where the lower temperature used in the formation of NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> for the SC2 method minimizes NaCl evaporation on the furnace outlet. While the SC1 method will require additional modifications for industrial use, such as nonporous furnace insulation, condensers, and appropriate flow controls, none of these are unreasonable should the performance benefit of the material be sufficient.</p>
               
               <p>The particle morphology yielded from the traditional solid-state route for synthesizing NM12 is presented in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a</a> (Supporting Information) as PC 900&nbsp;C 10&nbsp;h, where PC is used to indicate polycrystalline material and the remaining details reference calcination temperature and hold time for the sample. Here, the same unmilled NiO/Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> powders shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information) were used again. Platelet-like morphology can be observed in the PC 900&nbsp;C 10&nbsp;h-material, although there is significant agglomeration and the hexagonal facets are not well defined. XRD data for NM12 synthesized with one calcination step (both PC- and SC1-route materials) are presented in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information). The PC material formed without prior ball-milling of the transition-metal oxides shows poor chemical conversion to NM12 with a NiO impurity of 8.8&nbsp;wt% (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). With milled oxides (2&nbsp;h in isopropanol at 400&nbsp;rpm), the conversion is greatly improved with a marginal 0.9&nbsp;wt% NiO impurity remaining. Some agglomeration is still present within this sample, although to a much lesser extent (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10b</a>, Supporting Information). In-depth discussion on the morphological dependance with time, temperature, and flux salt composition are discussed in the Supporting Information, including particle size distributions (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information) and the use of an Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> flux (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
               </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202408642-sec-0070">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="adfm202408642-sec-0070-title">2.1.4 Comparison of Methods and Electrochemical Performance</h4>
               
               <p>Neither the SC1 nor the SC2 method proved to significantly alter particle morphology for NM12 (i.e., forming polyhedra versus platelets versus octahedra, <i>etc</i>.), but these steps and processes may prove more useful for other sodium layered oxide compositions. It was shown that simply increasing the salt content with an SC1 method for Na<sub>0.66</sub>Ni<sub>0.26</sub>Zn<sub>0.07</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> resulted in larger size and preferential growth of the {010} planes toward polyhedral morphology instead of platelets.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0034_R_d20949610e1089" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
               
               <p>A schematic summary comparing the synthesis methods used is presented as <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0002">2a</a>. For this work, one material was selected from each synthesis route for electrochemical comparison based on their degree of agglomeration, ease of synthesis/processing, and purity of material with respect to NiO. For the standard solid-state synthesis route, this material is the PC 900&nbsp;C 10&nbsp;h (milled) powder, now denoted as <i>PC</i>. For <i>SC1</i>, this is the SC1 900&nbsp;C  10 h material, and for <i>SC2</i> this is the NaCl (unmilled) material. Micrographs for these materials are presented in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0002">2bd</a>.</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="adfm202408642-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/38dd1ed6-a03e-4e0c-b3c3-01f1ab8a3815/adfm202408642-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/38dd1ed6-a03e-4e0c-b3c3-01f1ab8a3815/adfm202408642-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f49d0ebd-27be-406a-be6d-34baa03660a9/adfm202408642-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/38dd1ed6-a03e-4e0c-b3c3-01f1ab8a3815/adfm202408642-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202408642-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202408642" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic summarization of the single crystal synthesis methods explored for Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. SEM micrographs for the materials considered for electrochemical characterization: b) <i>PC</i>, c) <i>SC1</i>, and d) <i>SC2</i>.</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               <p>The lack of tunability in particle morphology for the SC1 route is likely due to two main factors: the propensity of the P3-phase Na<sub>x</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (which precedes the P2 phase during calcination) to crystallize as platelets below the salt flux melting temperature, and the subsequent surface free energies between both the P3 and P2 phases and the molten NaCl. This is shown by the SEM images presented in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information) and the XRD patterns in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information), where materials from each synthesis route undergo different calcination times/temperatures of either 825&nbsp;C for 3&nbsp;h or a ramp to 900&nbsp;C with no hold, as opposed to the traditional 10&nbsp;h hold at 900&nbsp;C for calcination of this material. These sequences allow for observations on the morphological evolution of the material before, during, and after the P2 phase forms, given that the morphology is not expected to change during cooling. Growth before P3 phase formation is minimal and the samples retain their initial morphology, based on which the spinel precursor was used.</p>
               
               <p>Using smaller NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> material (0.4&nbsp;m versus 3.4&nbsp;m) in the SC2 synthesis method shows increased conversion to the P2 phase during a 1&nbsp;C min<sup>1</sup> ramp to 900&nbsp;C (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14c,d</a>, Supporting Information), suggesting that the less defined platelet morphology seen in the <i>SC2</i> material is due to kinetic limitations for particle growth before the P2 phase forms. NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> is likely a more stable intermediate with slower transition-metal diffusion, which will dictate particle growth. This kinetic limitation prevents the unmilled <i>SC2</i> material from forming defined platelets in the P3 phase during calcination, which is corroborated by the SEM image in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14g</a> (Supporting Information). After a 3&nbsp;h hold at 825&nbsp;C, the particles have non-uniform morphology and are fused together in Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>. For both <i>PC</i> and <i>SC1</i>, platelet like morphology can already be observed at 825&nbsp;C (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14e,f</a>, Supporting Information). This suggests that the phase transformation from NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> to P3-Na<sub>x</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and subsequent particle growth is more kinetically limited compared to the <i>PC</i> and <i>SC1</i> materials. With small enough NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> particles the kinetic limitations can be gradually overcome at 900&nbsp;C as the P2-NM12 phase will similarly grow platelets. This mechanism is likely responsible for the reduction in hard agglomerates seen in the <i>SC2</i> material. Further studies with <i>in-situ</i> (heating) synchrotron XRD and computational analysis with ab initio molecular dynamics (AIMD) would be required to corroborate these observations,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0047_R_d20949610e1229" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Nat. Mater.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> although the discussed principles can prove useful for other spinel compositions and their applications to sodium layered oxides.</p>
               
               <p>Morphologies for intermediate time/temperature calcinations for <i>PC</i> and <i>SC1</i> are similar to each other, suggesting that the NaCl flux does not significantly alter the surface free energy minimization of the different crystal facets during P3-phase formation. However, particle size after a 1&nbsp;C min<sup>1</sup> ramp to 900&nbsp;C is smaller on average for <i>SC1</i>, which is consistent with the dissolution of the P3 material into the molten-salt. These findings are consistent with the XRD patterns presented in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information), where kinetic limitations in the unmilled <i>SC2</i> translate to minimal conversion from P3 to P2 after 3&nbsp;h at 825&nbsp;C. Comparatively, both <i>PC</i> and <i>SC1</i> materials show some degree of P2 formation under those conditions. Examining <i>PC</i> and <i>SC1</i> XRD patterns for both intermediate conditions suggests that P2 formation occurs more slowly within the molten-salt flux, preventing hard agglomerations from forming within the <i>SC1</i> material.</p>
               
               <p>It is also interesting to note that the <i>SC2</i> material appeared to significantly benefit from the deviation of hexagonal platelets in terms of tap density, with tap density measurements as follows: <i>PC</i> = 1.47&nbsp;g mL<sup>1</sup>, <i>SC1</i> = 1.42&nbsp;g mL<sup>1</sup>, and <i>SC2</i> = 1.88&nbsp;g mL<sup>1</sup>. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0003">3</a> is provided to compare the extent of agglomeration within the pristine electrodes, as electrode processing may break up softer agglomerates. Due to the documented egregious particle cracking within NM12, it would be difficult to discern grain boundaries this way in cycled cathodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0012_R_d20949610e1285" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nano Energy">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> There are clearly larger agglomerates with internal grains within the <i>PC</i> material (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0003">3ac</a>), whereas <i>SC1</i> and <i>SC2</i> particles are more isolated in the carbon-binder matrix, shown by the dark regions present between active material particles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0003">3di</a>). Surface areas of these three materials were measured with BET as follows (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information): <i>PC</i> = 1.42 m<sup>2</sup>g<sup>1</sup> (average particle size = 1.9&nbsp;m), <i>SC1</i> = 1.69 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>1</sup> (average particle size = 2.8&nbsp;m), <i>SC2</i> = 1.58 m<sup>2</sup>g<sup>1</sup> (average particle size = 2.7&nbsp;m). The larger surface area values for the single-crystal materials, despite having 50% larger average particle size, further emphasizes the differences in agglomeration compared to the baseline <i>PC</i> material.</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="adfm202408642-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a5d1383c-2990-444e-bf5e-d7f9201995d3/adfm202408642-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/a5d1383c-2990-444e-bf5e-d7f9201995d3/adfm202408642-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4d54fb81-c68a-44ef-8b56-ed6a1374daa2/adfm202408642-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a5d1383c-2990-444e-bf5e-d7f9201995d3/adfm202408642-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202408642-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202408642" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Back-scattered electron (BSE)-SEM micrographs of pristine electrode cross-sections for ac) <i>PC</i>, df) <i>SC1</i>, and g-i) <i>SC2</i> materials.</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               <p><b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0004">4a</a> shows the electrochemical cycling performances of <i>SC1</i> and <i>SC2</i> materials compared to the solid-state synthesized baseline material <i>PC</i>. The initial coulombic efficiency is 85.3% for the <i>PC</i> material, compared to 88.2% for the <i>SC1</i> and 90.4% for the <i>SC2</i> materials. This results in an initial discharge capacity that is roughly marginally higher for both single-crystal materials when compared to the baseline PC material. This difference doubles for <i>SC1</i> and <i>SC2</i> after 5 cycles, after which the difference in capacity roughly stabilizes. The <i>PC</i> material falls below 80% capacity retention within 12 cycles compared to 18 and 19 cycles for <i>SC1</i> and <i>SC2</i> materials, respectively. This sharp initial decrease in capacity has been well reported in the literature for samples synthesized with the baseline synthesis procedure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0012_R_d20949610e1386" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nano Energy">12</a>, <a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0013_R_d20949610e1386" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Chem. Commun.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The combination of these factors suggests that the additional capacity loss experienced by the <i>PC</i> material can be attributed to loss of accessible active material through intragranular cracking in the secondary particle agglomerates that are present in the <i>PC</i> baseline material.</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="adfm202408642-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/26445be3-15af-49e8-8284-126066ac0999/adfm202408642-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/26445be3-15af-49e8-8284-126066ac0999/adfm202408642-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5d52a03c-19e2-49a8-9f76-c07849731686/adfm202408642-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/26445be3-15af-49e8-8284-126066ac0999/adfm202408642-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202408642-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202408642" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Cycling performances of <i>SC1</i> and <i>SC2</i> materials compared to the baseline <i>PC</i> material against sodium-metal anode. Voltage profile evolution over the first 30 cycles (cycle number included in key) for b) the <i>PC</i> baseline material, c) the <i>SC1</i> material, and d) the <i>SC2</i> material. Deconvolution of capacity source during the first 30 cycles for the <i>PC</i>, <i>SC1</i>, and <i>SC2</i> materials: e) evolution of higher voltage capacity over cycling, f) evolution of lower voltage capacity over cycling, and (g) overpotential growth.</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               <p>Examining the voltage profiles in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0004">4bd</a> shows a trend previously observed on single-crystal Na<sub>x</sub>MO<sub>2</sub>: sharper plateaus for the single crystal <i>SC1</i> and <i>SC2</i> materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0024_R_d20949610e1449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Mater.">24</a>, <a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0028_R_d20949610e1449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The discharge plateau corresponding to the P2  O2 phase transition is also better preserved in the single-crystal materials. The polarization growth on both charge and discharge becomes evident for the <i>PC</i> baseline after 15 cycles, which can be more clearly seen in the normalized voltage profiles in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
               
               <p>Lastly, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0004">4e,f</a> shows a deconstruction of the capacity sources by voltage window for the three materials by using the voltage profiles and differential capacity curves presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0004">4</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> (Supporting Information), respectively. Here, the Higher Voltage region refers to capacity from the P2  O2 phase transition, which fades more rapidly for the <i>PC</i> material than <i>SC1</i> and <i>SC2</i> in the initial cycles. After the first cycle, there is a difference of 3.5&nbsp;mA h g<sup>1</sup>, which grows to 6&nbsp;mA h g<sup>1</sup> after 5 cycles and stabilizes until 15 cycles. Over the first 15 cycles, attributing the difference in Higher Voltage capacity between polycrystalline (<i>PC</i>) and single-crystal materials to loss of accessible active material results in nearly identical Lower Voltage capacity for all three materials. The excellent kinetics associated with the Lower Voltage contribution to the capacity of NM12 means the same Lower Voltage capacity should be observed for polycrystalline and single-crystal materials given the same amount of accessible active material.</p>
               
               <p>Capacity loss due to polarization growth becomes a more significant issue after 15 cycles, evidenced as well by Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0004">4g</a> and the relative change in dQ/dV curves in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> (Supporting Information). This corroborates the hypothesis that over the initial cycles when cracking is known to be prominent, the <i>PC</i> material experiences greater loss of active material due to the presence of internal grains within agglomerates that are isolated from the carbon-binder matrix. Cycling performance of <i>SC1</i> and <i>SC2</i> in the voltage range of 2.4  4&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>, where particle cracking does <i>not</i> occur, is not significantly different from those of published reports with polycrystalline materials due to the electrochemomechanical stability of NM12 below 4&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0011_R_d20949610e1508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Storage Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
               </section>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202408642-sec-0080">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202408642-sec-0080-title">2.2 Localized Saturated Electrolyte (LSE) and Surface Stability</h3>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202408642-sec-0090">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="adfm202408642-sec-0090-title">2.2.1 Electrolyte Formulation</h4>
               
               <p>A localized saturated electrolyte composition was employed to suppress solvent decomposition at the cathode interface. While LHCEs have become commonplace in the literature for lithium-metal batteries, the traditional solvent dimethoxyethane (DME) is not stable up to 4.3&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> even within the LHCE structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0038_R_d20949610e1529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - ACS Energy Lett.">38</a>, <a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0039_R_d20949610e1529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Instead, concentrated solutions of NaPF<sub>6</sub> in propylene carbonate can be diluted with 1,1,2,2- tetrafluoroethyl-2,2,3,3-tetrafluoropropylether (TTE), which is non-solvating and does not strongly interact with carbonate solvents.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0037_R_d20949610e1536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Energy Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Two different effective concentrations were created: one starting from 3&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> and the other 2.2&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub>, both utilizing 97&nbsp;wt% propylene carbonate and 3&nbsp;wt% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) as the initial solvent. The two electrolytes were diluted at a 1: 1 ratio with TTE to create effective concentrations of 1.5&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> and 1.1&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub>, respectively. The localized solvation structure is confirmed with Fourier-transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a> (Supporting Information), which shows an increase in the relative fraction of coordinated PF<sub>6</sub><sup></sup> anions in the wavenumber region of 800  900 cm<sup>1</sup> and a similar increase in the coordination of propylene carbonate in the 700 to 750 cm<sup>1</sup> region.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0037_R_d20949610e1561" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Energy Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The conductivity of the 2.2&nbsp;M LSE was slightly lower at 4.64 mS cm<sup>1</sup> compared to 6.09 mS cm<sup>1</sup> for the baseline electrolyte. This confirms the design principle of a localized saturated electrolyte with NaPF<sub>6</sub> salt that forms CIPs/AGGs and increases the coordination between NaPF<sub>6</sub> and propylene carbonate relative to the baseline 1&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in propylene carbonate/FEC electrolyte.</p>
               </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202408642-sec-0100">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="adfm202408642-sec-0100-title">2.2.2 Electrochemical Performance</h4>
               
               <p>Coin half-cells were prepared with the <i>SC1</i>-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathodes discussed in the previous section and the baseline electrolyte (1&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in 97&nbsp;wt% propylene carbonate and 3&nbsp;wt% FEC), 2.2&nbsp;M LSE and 3&nbsp;M LSE electrolytes. The <i>SC1</i> material (which will be simplified to SC for the remainder of this work) was selected due to its relative ease of processing in a laboratory setting when compared to <i>SC2</i>, considering that both the <i>SC1</i> and <i>SC2</i> cathodes previously discussed showed nearly identical performance with the baseline electrolyte. The results of electrochemical cycling at C/5 (C = 140&nbsp;mA h g<sup>1</sup>) are shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0005">5</a>. Both LSE electrolytes show significantly improved capacity retention after 50 cycles (80% for the 2.2&nbsp;M LSE and 77% for the 3&nbsp;M LSE) compared to only 58% retention for the baseline electrolyte with the SC cathodes. The improvement offered by using a more stable electrolyte is several times greater than the improvement offered by stabilizing the secondary particle structure through the use of single-crystal active material, as demonstrated by the comparison of the baseline SC and PC materials in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0005">5a</a>.</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="adfm202408642-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/eca4e160-9cbc-4e21-95ce-4362c95e1ba4/adfm202408642-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/eca4e160-9cbc-4e21-95ce-4362c95e1ba4/adfm202408642-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/46a7cbc8-7c19-459c-9a22-4235057098aa/adfm202408642-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/eca4e160-9cbc-4e21-95ce-4362c95e1ba4/adfm202408642-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202408642-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202408642" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Cycling performances of the single crystal (SC) and polycrystalline (PC) Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathodes in different electrolytes, at C/5 rate at 2.5  4.35&nbsp;V against Na metal. Evolution of capacity versus voltage profiles over 50 cycles for b) SC material with baseline electrolyte c) PC material with baseline electrolyte, d) SC material with 2.2&nbsp;M LSE electrolyte, and e) SC material with 3&nbsp;M LSE electrolyte. f) Sodium-metal symmetric cell cycling at 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>2</sup> and 1&nbsp;mA h cm<sup>2</sup> with the different electrolytes.</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               <p>Pairing the PC material with the 2.2&nbsp;M LSE does, however, corroborate the observed trend with capacity loss due to the isolation of internal grains, where the difference in capacity retention after 50 cycles between PC and SC electrodes is roughly 7% in each electrolyte. Overpotentials on the first cycle are: 250&nbsp;mV for 2.2&nbsp;M LSE (PC), 200&nbsp;mV for 2.2&nbsp;M LSE (SC), 155&nbsp;mV for Baseline (SC), and 165&nbsp;mV for Baseline (PC). The evolution of voltage profiles over cycling for the 2.2&nbsp;M LSE (PC) sample is included as Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21a</a> (Supporting Information) with a comparison of overpotential growth for the two different 2.2&nbsp;M LSE samples as Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21b</a> (Supporting Information). While the initial overpotential is higher across all LSE samples, the overpotential decreases over the first 5 to 10 cycles to a minimum of 120&nbsp;mV for both the PC and SC electrodes. This is contrary to the polarization growth seen for the baseline electrolyte in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0004">4g</a>, which increases monotonically across all materials, surpassing the overpotential of the LSE electrolytes by the fifth cycle. Electrolyte viscosity and poor wetting of the active material could be partially responsible for the high initial overpotential observed for the LSE samples, though it is more likely due to the anode electrolyte interface (AEI) formation on sodium metal during plating in the initial charge steps. This will be discussed later alongside full cell cycling data.</p>
               
               <p>Differences in polarization are further evidenced by the voltage profiles in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0005">5be</a>, where discharge on the 50th cycle is initiated below 3.75&nbsp;V for the samples with the baseline electrolyte while the LSE samples start above 4&nbsp;V even after 50 cycles. The plateaus associated with the P2  O2 phase transition are better maintained in the LSE electrolyte, indicating more homogenous and less impeded phase transitions across the electrodes over cycling, which is emphasized by the dQ dV<sup>1</sup>&nbsp;versus V peaks above 4&nbsp;V shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a> (Supporting Information). The cycling data suggests that the more coordinated carbonate solvents within the LSE electrolyte are less reactive with the electrode surface and specifically the lattice oxygen.</p>
               
               <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0005">5f</a> shows the cycling of Na | Na symmetric cells at an areal current density of 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>2</sup> and areal capacity of 1&nbsp;mA h cm<sup>2</sup>. Under these conditions, the 3&nbsp;M LSE shows worse stability than the baseline electrolyte with only 250&nbsp;h of stable cycling for the former compared to 500&nbsp;h for the latter. This is likely due to the salt within the electrolyte being close to its saturation limit, where eventual decomposition of the carbonate solvent will result in supersaturation of NaPF<sub>6</sub> and the salt will crash out, creating micro-shorts in the cell.
