However, the 2.2&nbsp;M LSE shows drastically improved performance compared to the baseline, with over 1200&nbsp;h of stable Na | Na cycling under these conditions. The lower overpotential and increased cycle life suggest that the Na metal forms a more stable interface with a decomposition of the LSE electrolyte.</p>
               </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202408642-sec-0110">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="adfm202408642-sec-0110-title">2.2.3 Characterization</h4>
               
               <p>Operando-XRD was performed to investigate any differences in the structural evolution of the cathode material during cycling that might correspond to improved mechanical stability of the electrode. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0006">6a,b</a> shows nearly identical phase behavior during cycling, specifically during the detrimental P2  O2 phase transition. This is reflected in the lattice parameter evolution (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0006">6c</a>), showing that the LSE electrolyte does not provide any mechanical stability improvement to NM12 by reducing volumetric expansion or strain within the lattice. These observations infer that the improvement in capacity retention does not follow from bulk particle stabilization.</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="adfm202408642-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/507274bc-b228-46c7-b6f3-f4802e48dce3/adfm202408642-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/507274bc-b228-46c7-b6f3-f4802e48dce3/adfm202408642-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/93070d82-640a-403b-8ae2-36ce0cb632f0/adfm202408642-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/507274bc-b228-46c7-b6f3-f4802e48dce3/adfm202408642-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202408642-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202408642" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Operando XRD patterns for single-crystal NM12 with a) the baseline electrolyte and b) 2.2&nbsp;M LSE electrolyte cycled at 2.5  4.5&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. c) Evolution of lattice parameters during charge/discharge for both electrolytes, showing both the P2 and O2 phases. d) Post-mortem XRD patterns from NM12 electrodes after extended cycling (50 cycles) with emphasis on the (002) and (012) reflections.</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0006">6d</a> shows post-mortem XRD performed on NM12 electrodes after 50 cycles. Here, the (002) peak at 15.8 (2) is noticeably broader for the electrode cycled within the LSE electrolyte compared to the baseline sample, while the (012) peak for both cycled electrodes remains nearly identical. The difference in peak ratios, specifically the lower value in (002)<sub>LSE</sub>/ (002)<sub>Baseline</sub> (= 0.952) relative to (012)<sub>LSE</sub>/ (012)<sub>Baseline</sub> (= 0.980), is consistent with a greater extent of transition metal/sodium mixing. Typically, transition-metal mixing is seen as deleterious as the transition-metal ion within the sodium layer limits capacity and can serve to increase the tortuosity of the diffusion pathway.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0048_R_d20949610e1741" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Sci. Bull.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, cation mixing can also improve structural stability because having transition-metal ions in the sodium planes effectively pin structural units and helps prevent undesirable phase transformations. Here, greater transition-metal mixing can be expected because the LSE cycles through much greater cumulative capacity from the O2 phase over its lifetime, where mixing arises due to a collapse of the <i>c</i>-parameter. Due to the relatively small difference in peak ratios and the preserved plateau-like behavior of the P2  O2 transition in the voltage profiles in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0005">5</a>, there is not a significant benefit to structural stability from an increase in cation mixing in this case.</p>
               
               <p>Focused ion-beam (FIB-) SEM was performed on cathodes cycled 50 times with the 2.2&nbsp;M LSE and baseline electrolytes to compare the extent of particle cracking in each. Surprisingly, the LSE cathode showed a similar, if not greater extent of, intragranular particle cracking compared to the baseline electrode (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0007">7ad</a>). An increase in particle cracking would be consistent with the observed trend in capacity retention if cracking is not the main cause for capacity fading. Greater utilization of sodium during cycling of the P2  O2 transition would initiate more stacking faults that result in layer exfoliation within NM12 single crystals. This again shows that bulk stabilization of NM12 is not the root cause for improved capacity retention with the LSE electrolyte.</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="adfm202408642-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/411a2382-3599-421f-a26e-1bebf95d25f2/adfm202408642-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/411a2382-3599-421f-a26e-1bebf95d25f2/adfm202408642-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4a9e41d4-e5af-4ece-9141-465d3be6e040/adfm202408642-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/411a2382-3599-421f-a26e-1bebf95d25f2/adfm202408642-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202408642-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202408642" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">FIB-SEM micrographs of SC1 electrode cross-sections for a) the baseline electrolyte and b) 2.2&nbsp;M LSE electrolyte after 50 cycles. c,d) Magnified particles within the SC1 electrodes for the baseline electrolyte and the 2.2&nbsp;M LSE electrolyte, respectively. Post-mortem cross-sectional SEM micrographs on the Na-metal anodes after 50 cycles for the e) baseline electrolyte and f) the 2.2&nbsp;M LSE electrolyte. Optical images of the sodium metal at the glass fiber interface (left) and the glass fiber separator (right) after cycling in the baseline electrolyte g) and the 2.2&nbsp;M LSE electrolyte h).</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               <p>Post-mortem cross-sectional SEM was also performed on the cycled Na-metal anodes for both electrolytes. The visible difference in contrast for the AEI and pristine sodium regions of the cycled Na metal in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0007">7e</a> is confirmed with energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX) in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a> (Supporting Information). Fluorine and phosphorus indicate salt decomposition products, and the silicon mapping is included to distinguish the separator interface from the AEI. Here, the AEI also tends to stick to the glass fiber separator creating some variance in the measured penetration depth of the reacted sodium region, which is roughly 230&nbsp;m for the baseline sample. Comparatively, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0007">7f</a> for the Na metal cycled in LSE electrolyte shows a smaller reacted layer of roughly 180&nbsp;m, despite cycling through greater cumulative capacity during its 50 cycles. The depth of the layer is confirmed by the EDX mappings shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a> (Supporting Information). The baseline Na metal shows a larger difference in the intensity of the carbon signal from the AEI to unreacted region when compared to the Na metal from the LSE sample. This suggests that the AEI composition is more organic in nature for the baseline electrolyte compared to the LSE electrolyte. Both samples were exposed to air concurrently, thus the extent to which the bare Na metal formed Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> should be the same across samples, but further evidence is needed to claim a more inorganic AEI is formed on the LSE sample. Optically, the images presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0007">7g,h</a> show that the sodium-separator and separator-cathode interfaces are similar, and that HF catalyzed discoloration of the glass fiber separators is minimal in both instances.</p>
               
               <p>To further probe the surface chemistry, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was performed on both pairs of NM12 cathodes and the AEI from Na-metal anodes. The XPS spectra from the cycled cathodes is presented in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0008">8ad</a> with emphasis on the C 1s, F 1s, O 1s, and P 2p regions. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0008">8e</a> compares the relative sensitivity factor (RSF) adjusted areas and deconvolutes the inorganic and organic components based on the observed spectra. The comparison of C 1s spectra shows that the baseline electrolyte has more varied decomposition products. More of the carbon area in the baseline spectra comes from peaks with binding energies greater than 288&nbsp;eV, which are associated with oxygenated/fluorinated organic molecules containing COH, CO, CO<sub>3</sub>, or CF bonds. The F 1s spectra are similar for both samples, although the LSE sample shows a relatively larger proportion of area attributed to the 685.8&nbsp;eV peak, which corresponds to the desirable cathode electrolyte interface (CEI) component of NaF. Comparison of the O 1s spectra suggests similar surface chemistry as the C 1s spectra, where a larger proportion of the area within the baseline sample comes from oxygen peaks with higher binding energies. The peak associated with lattice oxygen in NM12 makes up a much larger proportion of the oxygen area in the LSE sample than in the baseline sample as well, suggesting a thinner CEI. Finally, the baseline sample has a much broader peak than the LSE sample in the P 2p energy region. This could be associated with the coexistence of one or more oxygenated phosphorus containing decomposition products from NaPF<sub>6</sub>, although these are difficult to deconvolute with the low relative intensity of the phosphorus signals.</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="adfm202408642-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0b3accb1-b0d8-4ab6-9373-e532c5823271/adfm202408642-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/0b3accb1-b0d8-4ab6-9373-e532c5823271/adfm202408642-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ded66921-2d53-4d2f-a086-d93782600e89/adfm202408642-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0b3accb1-b0d8-4ab6-9373-e532c5823271/adfm202408642-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 8<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202408642-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202408642" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Comparative XPS data for the CEI on cycled SC Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathodes. XPS data of the a) C 1s, b) F 1s, c) O 1s, and d) P 2p after 50 cycles. Measurement data provided in red with colored regions representing Lorentzian/Gaussian fits for individual components and the black line for the cumulative peak fit. e) Deconvolution of atomic percentage derived from XPS measurements with blue bars representing organic components of the CEI and orange bars representing inorganic components.</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               <p>Somewhat surprisingly, the LSE sample has a larger overall proportion of carbon containing decomposition products (55.9% compared to 45.9%). However, the more important factor appears to be the comparative proportion of oxygen present in the two samples. The relative proportion of oxygen present from organic products in the LSE sample is only 24.8% compared to 31.3% for the baseline electrolyte. Lattice oxygen is also present in the LSE sample at a higher proportion of 2% compared to only 0.5% for the baseline sample. Oxygen must come from one of two sources: either decomposition of propylene carbonate/FEC, or from NM12 (the oxygen content in TTE is negligible). Removing lattice oxygen would result in defects and decomposition products that exacerbate capacity fade, which would be consistent with the electrochemical cycling data and the observed overpotential growth during cycling. The XPS data suggest that the baseline electrolyte is less oxidatively stable, which would be supported by previous observations in the literature on the reactivity of oxygen at high voltages in sodium layered oxides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0049_R_d20949610e1850" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Adv. Mater.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The larger proportion of inorganic fluorine components present in the LSE sample suggest a more favorable contribution to the CEI composition as well.</p>
               
               <p>A combination of atomic resolution annular dark-field scanning transmission electron microscopy (ADF-STEM) and electron energy loss spectroscopy (EELS) have previously been conducted on cycled LiNiO<sub>2</sub> cathodes that utilized an analogous LSE electrolyte at high voltage (corresponding to 4.1&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). The results showed a much thinner surface reconstruction layer, demonstrating a reduction in oxygen loss through the electrolyte. Based on our results and characterization, the LSE used within this work likely gains the same benefits from the improvement to the electrolyte's oxidative stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0038_R_d20949610e1862" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - ACS Energy Lett.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
               
               <p>As for the AEI from the Na-metal anodes, the C 1s, F 1s, P 2p, and Na 1s spectral regions were analyzed with XPS (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0009">9ad</a>). In this case, the baseline electrolyte showed an additional carbon peak corresponding to CO bonding energy, with relatively higher proportions of carbon bonds present in forms other than CC bonds compared to the LSE sample. The presence of the CO bonding peak in the baseline sample indicates a greater reduction of propylene carbonate/FEC as the only possible source of oxygen on the anode side. The F 1s spectra are noticeably different for both samples, where the baseline has more varied fluorine containing decomposition products with a larger proportion being organic and the LSE sample shows a higher proportion of NaF bonding. The P 2p spectra is similarly more varied for the baseline electrolyte sample, although the relative signal is small. Lastly, the Na 1s spectra for the LSE sample shows an additional (small) peak corresponding to sodium carbonate.</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="adfm202408642-fig-0009"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1a7306fe-3c17-4561-9047-0fcc5d4ff825/adfm202408642-fig-0009-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/1a7306fe-3c17-4561-9047-0fcc5d4ff825/adfm202408642-fig-0009-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/904be6f6-02c2-4ff8-b2d1-6ff83694c8d6/adfm202408642-fig-0009-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1a7306fe-3c17-4561-9047-0fcc5d4ff825/adfm202408642-fig-0009-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 9<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202408642-fig-0009&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202408642" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Comparative XPS data for the AEI on cycled Na-metal anodes. XPS data for the a) C 1s, b) F 1s, c) P 2p, and d) Na 1s after 50 cycles. Measurement data provided in red with colored regions representing Lorentzian/Gaussian fits for individual components and the black line for the cumulative peak fit. e) Deconvolution of atomic percentage derived from XPS measurements with blue bars representing organic components of the AEI and orange bars representing inorganic components.</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0009">9e</a> shows the deconvolution of RSF adjusted areas of organic and inorganic components of the AEI. A larger overall proportion of the signal from the baseline spectra is from carbon (89.2% compared to 79.3% for the LSE) with a much larger proportion coming from peaks other than the C-C peak. Inorganic and salt decomposition products are relatively small in comparison for the baseline electrolyte, with only 0.3% NaF compared to 2.2% NaF in the LSE sample. The LSE sample also contains substantially more Na, which would suggest an AEI with better diffusion, as evidenced by the relatively smaller overpotential growth within the electrochemical cycling of the LSE samples compared to the baseline.</p>
               </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202408642-sec-0120">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="adfm202408642-sec-0120-title">2.2.4 Full Cells and Rate Testing</h4>
               
               <p>To deconvolute the synergistic influences of CEI and AEI stabilization, full cells were assembled with both electrolytes and SC cathodes against Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) anodes to eliminate the influence of Na metal (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a>, Supporting Information). By design, the first V<sup>3+/2+</sup> redox reaction will be accessed at 1.7&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>, which has been well documented to have excellent cycling stability though at a cost of 10% of the initial capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0050_R_d20949610e1926" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Chem. Commun.">50</a>, <a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0051_R_d20949610e1926" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The SC-NM12 cathodes achieve 127&nbsp;mA h g<sup>1</sup> in both electrolytes against NVP, roughly 90% of what was demonstrated in half-cells (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0010">10a</a>). In this full cell configuration, the LSE electrolyte still demonstrates greatly improved capacity retention (80% retention at 25 cycles compared to only 54% retention for the baseline electrolyte), exemplifying the importance of the CEI stability. Interestingly, the larger initial overpotential seen with the LSE electrolytes in half cells is not observed in this full cell configurationboth electrolytes show 55&nbsp;mV of overpotential on the initial cycle (which would be closer to 150&nbsp;mV when accounting for first cycle loss due to the NVP anode). This suggests that the initial reaction between sodium metal and the LSE components creates an AEI composition that has sluggish diffusion, although repeated cycling results in a more stable interphase with better diffusion kinetics.</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="adfm202408642-fig-0010"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/14ee98d9-d4aa-4732-a09b-f22fd75fd867/adfm202408642-fig-0010-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/14ee98d9-d4aa-4732-a09b-f22fd75fd867/adfm202408642-fig-0010-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c1a35611-9335-4756-8818-4137f4d34ad4/adfm202408642-fig-0010-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/14ee98d9-d4aa-4732-a09b-f22fd75fd867/adfm202408642-fig-0010-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 10<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202408642-fig-0010&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202408642" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Full cell cycling data against Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> anodes for both electrolytes at 1.0  2.75&nbsp;V (roughly 2.65  4.4&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). b) Evolution of the voltage profiles for both electrolytes during full cell cycling. c) Rate performance testing against Na metal at a constant C/10 charge rate at 2.4  4. 3&nbsp;V, with d) corresponding voltage profiles for both electrolytes. GITT voltage versus time curves for e) the baseline electrolyte and f) the LSE electrolyte. g) Comparison of the first and second cycle GITT charge curves for both electrolytes.</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               <p>To ensure that the observed capacity fade is a consequence of cathodic degradation, a N: P ratio much larger than one was used. Presodiated NVP was also used in a separate test to confirm the observed trends with an anode containing excess sodium, with the presodiation procedure and details on full cell parameters described fully in the experimental section. This configuration allows for a full utilization of the cathode material (initial capacity of 142&nbsp;mA h g<sup>1</sup>), and the LSE electrolyte shows similar enhancements to cycle stability when excess sodium is contained in the NVP anode (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a>, Supporting Information). The results with full cells, coupled with the results from XPS, indicate a clear improvement in the capacity retention of NM12 owing to the better oxidative stability of the electrolyte resulting in a more advantageous CEI.</p>
               
               <p>Despite the obvious improvement in capacity retention, the LSE electrolyte offers a lower conductivity of 4.64 mS cm<sup>1</sup> compared to 6.09 mS cm<sup>1</sup> for the baseline electrolyte. Rate performance testing assessed the detriment of this lower conductivity and is presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0010">10c</a>. Clearly, the LSE electrolyte outperforms the baseline at higher rates, but this is partially due to the difference in capacity retention for the two electrolytes within the first 20 cycles. The LSE electrolyte achieves 122&nbsp;mA h g<sup>1</sup> at a high rate of 5C rate91% utilization compared to the C/10 cycle immediately after rate testing. The baseline electrolyte is only able to achieve as high as 102&nbsp;mA h g<sup>1</sup> at 5C rate, but this is 87% utilization when comparing the last 5C cycle to the C/10 cycle after rate testing. The polarization growth within the baseline electrolyte becomes more exaggerated at higher rates, emphasizing the improved chemical stability of NM12 with the LSE electrolyte across all rates (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0010">10d</a>). Galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was also performed on SC cathodes with the baseline and LSE electrolytes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0010">10e,f</a>). This shows that the kinetics on discharge for the two electrolytes are nearly identical as expected, with the diffusion coefficients presented in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27a,b</a> (Supporting Information). Although the first cycle charge is fairly impeded for the LSE electrolyte, a second charge cycle of GITT was performed for both electrolytes and the effect is greatly diminished (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27c</a>, Supporting Information). One possible explanation for this effect is that the SEI has better wettability after formation on charge, although the effect is not a significant detriment as it resolves over the course of one or two cycles. The change in polarization from first to second charge is strikingly clear in the IR drop for both materials displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202408642-fig-0010">10g</a>. Collectively, these results affirm that the benefits of the coordinated structure of the LSE electrolyte greatly outweigh the negative consequences of reduced ionic conductivity or wettability.</p>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202408642-sec-0130">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202408642-sec-0130-title">3 Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>Two molten-salt assisted synthesis pathways were investigated for their ability to form and control single-crystal morphology of P2-type Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. The first method made use of the mixed transition-metal spinel NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> as a precursor and demonstrated tunable particle morphology with the use of abundant and inexpensive flux salts. Inclusion of a flux salt greatly improved the conversion from NiO/Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> to NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, although tunability of the NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> precursor morphology did not translate well to the tunability of Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> morphology and size. However, the sodiation kinetics of NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> during the calcination of NM12 allows for reduction in agglomeration and a significant increase in tap density compared to the traditional solid-state synthesis with NiO/Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>.</p>
         
         <p>Selection of molten-salt flux is even more limited for the SC1 method with only NaCl being viable. Primary particle morphology remains consistent across different flux compositions and temperatures, forming single crystals as well-defined hexagonal platelets. Both the SC1 and SC2 synthesis methods discussed are effective at reducing the degree of agglomeration compared to the solid-state synthesized material and they replace the need for ball-milling or coprecipitation. The single-crystal active material particles used within this study deconvolute the capacity decay mechanisms of intragranular and intergranular particle cracking within Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, marginally improving capacity and capacity retention as roughly 5% of active material becomes isolated within polycrystalline agglomerates. The work offers motivation for deeper investigation into the sodiation mechanisms of transition metal spinel compositions, such as Ni<sub>1+x</sub>Mn<sub>2-x</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and beyond. Future work should also focus on understanding the parasitic electrolyte reactions at higher voltages as they relate to capacity retention and nucleation of microcracks in Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>.</p>
         
         <p>Furthermore, the design and implementation of a novel localized saturated electrolyte was demonstrated, for the first time, in a sodium-ion system. Electrochemical cycling with the LSE and SC-NM12 was shown to substantially improve the cycle life of NM12 at high voltages, with the benefit from the LSE far outweighing the benefit from single-crystal morphology alone. Cycle life to 80% retention improves from 12 to 18 cycles with the advancement from polycrystalline to single-crystal material, and then from 18 cycles to 50 cycles with the implementation of the LSE.</p>
         
         <p>The solvation structure of the reported LSE was shown to increase the oxidative stability of the salt-solvent pair. XPS on cycled cathodes demonstrated a greater proportion of advantageous NaF within the CEI composition, and notably lower organic oxygen content compared to the CEI formed with the standard 1&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in propylene carbonate/FEC electrolyte. Surface stabilization of the cathode interface was isolated with full cell cycling against Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, as XPS on the sodium-metal interface and Na | Na symmetric cell testing demonstrated a competing stabilization benefit of the LSE on the Na-metal side. Operando XRD and post-mortem cross-sectional SEM show that the bulk structural degradation mechanisms are the same within the LSE electrolyte as the baseline. In conclusion, the results presented demonstrate that microcracking is not the primary mode of failure, rather, the oxidative stability of the electrolyte and surface reactivity are the root causes that are only exacerbated by the inherent intragranular particle cracking issue that stems from the P2  O2 transition. Future research should focus on the solvation structure and surface reactivity of advanced electrolytes for high energy-density sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202408642-sec-0140">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202408642-sec-0140-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202408642-sec-0150">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202408642-sec-0160">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202408642-sec-0170">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202408642-sec-0170-title"> Material Synthesis</h4>
                  
                  <p><i>Polycrystalline (PC)</i>: For milled materials only, equimolar amounts of NiO and Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> were ball-milled together at 400&nbsp;rpm for 2&nbsp;h in isopropanol. The milled/unmilled mixed-oxide powder was added to an alumina crucible in a ratio of 1.05&nbsp;mol NaOH: 1&nbsp;mol NiO: 1&nbsp;mol Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, with the 5% excess NaOH to account for volatilization losses of the sodium source. The powder was then calcined at 900&nbsp;C for 10&nbsp;h (or 1000&nbsp;C for 6&nbsp;h) in air inside a box furnace. After first melting NaOH at 500&nbsp;C for 2&nbsp;h, the furnace was then ramped up to 900&nbsp;C (or 1000&nbsp;C) at a rate of 1&nbsp;C min<sup>1</sup>. The furnace was then switched off to allow for natural cooling.</p>
                  
                  <p><i>Single Crystals with a One-Step Synthesis (SC1)</i>: For this route, a single-step direct molten-salt assisted synthesis method was used to form Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. An equimolar ratio of NiO and Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> powders was mixed thoroughly with a mortar and pestle. Varying amounts of NaCl were added to the mixed oxide powder, ranging from 1  5 mols of NaCl per mol of NiO/Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> in the mixed oxide powder. The combined oxides and salt were placed inside an alumina crucible and NaOH was then added into the crucible at a ratio of 1.05&nbsp;mol NaOH: 1&nbsp;mol: NiO 1&nbsp;mol Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>. The crucible was placed in a box furnace in air that was then heated to 500&nbsp;C and held there for 3&nbsp;h to allow the NaOH to melt. Afterward, the temperature was increased at a rate of 1&nbsp;C min<sup>1</sup> until reaching the final temperature (either 825&nbsp;C, 900&nbsp;C, or 1000&nbsp;C). The final temperature was maintained for 0  10&nbsp;h depending on the sample, the furnace was then cooled at a rate of 1&nbsp;C min<sup>1</sup> down to 700&nbsp;C, and at a rate of 5&nbsp;C min<sup>1</sup> from 700&nbsp;C down to 300&nbsp;C, and then the sample was removed from the box furnace. Once the material had cooled to room temperature, it was washed thoroughly with water with vacuum filtration to remove any remaining NaCl. It was then dried and stored in an oven at 110&nbsp;C.</p>
                  
                  <p><i>Single Crystals with a Two-Step Synthesis (SC2)</i>: A two-step, indirect molten-salt assisted synthesis method was used for this material similar to our previous work.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0028_R_d20949610e2168" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the initial step, a 1: 1&nbsp;mol ratio of NiO and Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> was either mixed thoroughly with a mortar and pestle, or ball milled in isopropanol for 2&nbsp;h at 400&nbsp;rpm. Salt fluxes (either NaCl or different ratios of mixed Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> and Li<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>) were then mixed in with the prepared metal oxides and placed in the furnace with an alumina crucible. This synthesis was carried out in air with a heating schedule consisting of a 5&nbsp;C min<sup>1</sup> ramp rate up to 650&nbsp;C, followed by a 1&nbsp;C min<sup>1</sup> ramp rate up to 825&nbsp;C where it was held for 2&nbsp;h to form NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. After the hold at 825&nbsp;C, the material was cooled to 650 at a rate of 1&nbsp;C min<sup>1</sup> to allow for controlled recrystallization of the salt-flux. Once the box furnace cooled to 650&nbsp;C, the furnace was switched off and the material was allowed to cool naturally. No active sodium source was used during this initial step, and the materials prepared after this first step were washed with water and ethanol, filtered, and dried.</p>
                  
                  <p>The second step in the SC2 synthesis was a solid-state calcination step. NaOH solution was added to an alumina crucible containing the NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> materials formed in the first step in a ratio of 0.70&nbsp;mol NaOH: 1&nbsp;mol NiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (5% excess NaOH). The crucibles were placed in a box furnace in air that was heated to 500&nbsp;C and held there for 2&nbsp;h to allow for the NaOH to melt. Afterward, the temperature was increased at a rate of 1&nbsp;C min<sup>1</sup> until reaching the final temperature (either 825&nbsp;C or 900&nbsp;C). The temperature was then held for 0  10&nbsp;h depending on the sample and allowed to cool naturally after the high temperature hold was completed, giving the final product of Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>.</p>
                  
                  <p>Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> was synthesized through a process of ball milling, spray drying, and heat treatment under inert atmosphere. First, to a 80&nbsp;mL zirconia steel milling jar containing 75&nbsp;g of 5&nbsp;mm zirconia balls, the following were added: 9.52&nbsp;g of V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> (Strem, 95%), 18.60&nbsp;g of Na<sub>3</sub>P<sub>3</sub>O<sub>9</sub> (Sigma Aldrich,  95%), 3.64&nbsp;g of Na<sub>2</sub>HPO<sub>4</sub>12H<sub>2</sub>O (Acros Organics, 98.5%), 1.25&nbsp;g of maltodextrin (Sigma Aldrich, DE 47), 1.25&nbsp;g of lactose (ChemCenter, 99%), 2.0&nbsp;g of a 1.5 wt% aqueous solution of sodium carboxymethyl cellulose (Nippon Paper, Sunrose MAC 350-HC), and 22.0&nbsp;g of deionized water. The mixture was ball milled (Fritsch, Pulverisette 5) at 500&nbsp;rpm for 2&nbsp;h. The resulting slurry was then spray dried (LABFREEZ, SD-501) with a drying air flow rate of 18 SCFM, an inlet temperature of 130&nbsp;C, an outlet temperature of 110&nbsp;C, a 0.7&nbsp;mm diameter nozzle, a solution feed rate of 2&nbsp;mL min<sup>1</sup>, and an atomizer pressure of 0.15 mPa. The resulting precursor was placed into a graphite crucible and loaded into a tube furnace with 0.5 L min<sup>1</sup> of Argon flow. The furnace was heated at a rate of 10&nbsp;C min<sup>1</sup> to 350&nbsp;C and held for 1 hour, heated at 10&nbsp;C min<sup>1</sup> to 550&nbsp;C and held for 2&nbsp;h, heated at 10&nbsp;C min<sup>1</sup> to 850&nbsp;C and held for 8&nbsp;h, and then cooled naturally to room temperature. The resulting powder was passed through a 25&nbsp;m sieve to give the finished Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202408642-sec-0180">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202408642-sec-0180-title"> Materials Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>Powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) measurements were performed with a Miniflex 600 X-ray diffractometer with Cu K<i></i> radiation (<i></i> = 1.541740 ) at a scan rate of 2&nbsp; min<sup>1</sup> from a 2 angle of 10&nbsp; to 80&nbsp;. Rietveld refinement of the XRD patterns was carried out with GSAS-II.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0052_R_d20949610e2283" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - J. Appl. Crystallogr.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
                  
                  <p>Operando XRD was performed with an electrochemical cell design utilizing a beryllium window inside of a Rigaku Ultima IV X-Ray diffractometer with Cu K<i></i> (<i></i> = 1.541740 ) radiation. A 2.5&nbsp;m thick aluminum foil (Geistnote) was wrapped around the electrode-facing side of the beryllium to prevent corrosion. Electrode processing on top of the Al/Be window for single-crystal Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is particularly important, as drop-casting onto the window would cause heterogeneity across the thickness of the electrode that limits the extent of the reaction observed by X-rays. Dry-cast electrodes would similarly introduce diffusion limitations across the thickness of the electrode during cycling because of high tortuosity. This electrode-level limitation is introduced by the aspect ratio of single-crystal platelets, which are highly orientable, creating preferred stacking orientation within the electrode during repeated rolling and reducing the porosity close to zero. While the separator-facing surface of the electrode would be at the specified end potential of 4.5&nbsp;V, the surface on the X-ray side would lag significantly, making the O2 phase reflections unobservable as indicated by a truncated high-voltage plateau for the electrochemical cell. Free-standing electrodes were thus slurry cast onto a glass plate with an active material: binder: conductive carbon ratio of 75: 15: 10, allowing for uniformly thin electrodes (2&nbsp;mg cm<sup>2</sup>) with appropriate electrochemical performance for characterization with operando X-ray diffraction. Slurry preparation steps and chemical constituents are otherwise identical to the cathode preparation section discussed later. The cell was then cycled at C/20 from 2.5  4.5&nbsp;V and XRD patterns were taken successively at a scan rate of 2.5&nbsp; min<sup>1</sup> from a 2 angle of 10&nbsp; to 50&nbsp;.</p>
                  
                  <p>Particle morphologies were imaged on a Tescan Vega3 field emission scanning electron microscope (SEM). Cross-sectional electrode samples were prepared with a razor blade. ImageJ software was used for producing particle size distribution statistics, with 300 particles being measured for each condition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202408642-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202408642-bib-0053_R_d20949610e2310" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Nat. Methods">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Focused-ion beam (FIB-) SEM was performed on a Thermo Fisher Scios 2 DualBeam instrument, which allowed for in-situ milling of cross-sections and imaging with the scanning electron microscope. Cross-sectional Na-metal samples were prepared concurrently by cutting slivers from the cycled anodes with a razor blade under an Argon environment and sealing the anode slivers within two layers of airtight transfer containers. The airtight containers were opened only at the instrument to load the samples, however, a thin layer of carbonate still formed within the 10 s window to load the samples.</p>
                  
                  <p>Nitrogen physisorption measurements were collected on a Micromeritics Tristar II Plus 3030 from a relative pressure range (p/p<sub>0</sub>) of 0.06 to 0.995. Tap density of powders was measured with a Quantachrome Autotap AT2 after 30 minutes.</p>
                  
                  <p>X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was performed with a PHI VersaProbe4 Scanning XPS Microprobe with Al K radiation source (1486.6&nbsp;eV). Cathode samples were prepared by scraping cycled material off the Al current collectors to reduce spatial variation within the electrode and average the composition. Anode samples were similarly prepared by gently removing the interface from the sodium metal with tweezers. Separating the interface layer from the sodium metal was a requirement for the instrument to subvert issues with possible air exposure and off-gassing of the sodium metal, though this also ensured that the sample would be more representative of the average chemical composition. Samples were transferred into the XPS in an airtight holder.</p>
                  
                  <p>Fourier-transform infrared (FTIR) spectroscopy was performed with a Thermo Scientific Nicoleti-S5 instrument in ATR mode. 32 successive scans were collected under ambient conditions for each spectrum with 4cm<sup>1</sup> resolution from 500 to 3600 cm<sup>1</sup>.</p>
                  
                  <p>Ionic conductivity measurements were carried out with a Mettler-Toledo SevenCompact S230 conductivity meter with 2&nbsp;mL of electrolyte inside of 4&nbsp;mL dram vials.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202408642-sec-0190">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202408642-sec-0190-title"> Electrochemical Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>Cathode composites were prepared with a ratio of active material: Super P carbon: polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) binder = 92: 5: 3 by weight. Slurries were prepared in N-methyl-2-pyrolidone (NMP) and mixed until uniform with an appropriate viscosity in a Thinky centrifugal mixer at 2000&nbsp;rpm. Cathode slurries were then blade-casted onto carbon-coated Al foil sheets with 3&nbsp;mg cm<sup>2</sup> loading of active material. After drying at 110&nbsp;C, the cathode sheets were calendared to roughly 35% porosity with hot rollers at 80&nbsp;C. Cathodes were then punched into 12&nbsp;mm disks. For Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> electrodes, the active material ratio was instead 95: 2.5: 2.5, with an active mass loading of 20&nbsp;mg cm<sup>2</sup>. Sodium metal anodes were prepared by removing surface impurities with a razor blade, rolling the metal, and punching it into 1/2-inch disks. Half-cells were assembled within an argon-filled glovebox into CR2032 coin cells with glass fiber separators and 50&nbsp;L of electrolyte. For the baseline electrolyte, 1&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in 97&nbsp;wt% propylene carbonate and 3&nbsp;wt% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) was used. LSE electrolytes were made by taking concentrated NaPF<sub>6</sub> in propylene carbonate/FEC (either 3&nbsp;M or 2.2&nbsp;M) and adding equal parts by volume of 1,1,2,2- tetrafluoroethyl-2,2,3,3-tetrafluoropropylether (TTE) (Synquest, purity 99%). Cells were cycled at a rate of C/5 rate (C = 140&nbsp;mA h g<sup>1</sup>) from 2.5 to 4.35&nbsp;V unless otherwise specified.</p>
                  
                  <p>Na | Na symmetric cells were assembled into CR2032 coin cells with two 12&nbsp;mm Na metals disks on opposite sides of a glass fiber separator and filled with 50&nbsp;L of electrolyte. The cells were cycled at a constant current of 1&nbsp;mA for 1&nbsp;h before alternating polarity.</p>
                  
                  <p>Presodiation of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> was carried out electrochemically within Swagelok cell casings with the baseline electrolyte and by discharging NVP at a rate of C/5 (1 C = 60&nbsp;mA h g<sup>1</sup>, corresponding to the available capacity from the 1.7&nbsp;V plateau versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). Electrode masses were 20&nbsp;mg cm<sup>2</sup>, giving an approximate N: P ratio close to 3 in full cells with NM12. Approximately 20&nbsp;mA h g<sup>1</sup> was discharged into the NVP electrodes, such that when paired with the NM12 electrodes the NVP would remain at a constant voltage within the middle third of its 1.7&nbsp;V plateau. The presodiated NVP electrodes were then removed and washed with propylene carbonate to remove any excess salt from the surface before being used for full cells. Full cells without a presodiation step made use of the same electrodes and N: P ratios.</p>
                  
                  <p>Galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was performed with alternating C/10, 5-minute current pulses, and 20-minute rest steps from 2.5 to 4.3&nbsp;V versus Na metal. The equation used to calculate Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients is included in the Supporting Information.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
